href="#cite_note-europa2011-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr class="mergedbottomrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Per capita</div></th><td>\n\xe2\x82\xac22,400<sup id="cite_ref-europa2011_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europa2011-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a>&#160;<span style="font-weight:normal;">(nominal)</span></th><td>\n2014&#160;estimate</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Total</div></th><td>\n\xe2\x82\xac42&#160;billion<sup id="cite_ref-auto_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr class="mergedbottomrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Per capita</div></th><td>\n\xe2\x82\xac23,400<sup id="cite_ref-auto_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Currency</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">Pound&#160;sterling</a> (<a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">GBP</a>;&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">\xc2\xa3</a>)</td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row">Time zone</th><td>\n<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a>&#8288;</span> (<a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">Greenwich Mean Time</a>)</td></tr><tr class="mergedbottomrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">\xe2\x80\xa2&#160;Summer&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time" title="Daylight saving time">DST</a>)</div></th><td>\n<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">UTC</a>+1</span> (<a href="/wiki/British_Summer_Time" title="British Summer Time">British Summer Time</a>)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Date format</th><td>\ndd/mm/yyyy&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a>)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Left-_and_right-hand_traffic" title="Left- and right-hand traffic">Driving side</a></th><td>\nleft</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Telephone_numbers_in_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Telephone numbers in Northern Ireland">Calling code</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Telephone_numbers_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Telephone numbers in the United Kingdom">+44</a><sup class="reference" id="ref_footnote_c"><a href="#endnote_footnote_c">[c]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<div style="text-align:left;margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha; margin-left: 1em; font-size:90%;">\n<li value="1">The official flag of Northern Ireland is the <a href="/wiki/Union_Jack" title="Union Jack">Union Jack</a> <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Banner" title="Ulster Banner">Ulster Banner</a> was used by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament of Northern Ireland</a> from 1953 to 1973 and is still used by some organisations and entities, and is seen as the unofficial flag of the region by <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionism in Northern Ireland">unionists</a> but its use is controversial. See <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Flag of Northern Ireland">Flag of Northern Ireland</a> for more.</li><li value="2"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_footnote_b"><b><a href="#ref_footnote_b">^</a></b></span>   Northern Ireland has no official language. English serves as the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> language of government and diplomacy and has been established through precedent. Irish and Ulster Scots are officially recognised by <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Government">Her Majesty\'s Government</a> as <a href="/wiki/Minority_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Minority languages">minority languages</a>.</li><li value="3"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_footnote_c"><b><a href="#ref_footnote_c">^</a></b></span>  +44 is always followed by 28 when calling landlines. The code is 028 within the UK and 048 from the Republic of Ireland where it is treated as a domestic call.</li>\n</ol></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Northern Ireland</b> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga">Tuaisceart \xc3\x89ireann</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Irish" title="Help:IPA/Irish">[\xcb\x88t\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0u\xc9\x99\xca\x83c\xc9\x99\xc9\xbe\xcb\xa0t\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0 \xcb\x88e\xcb\x90\xc9\xbe\xca\xb2\xc9\x99n\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0]</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Tuaisceart_Eireann.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Tuaisceart_Eireann.ogg" class="internal" title="Tuaisceart Eireann.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>;<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster-Scots</a>: <i><i lang="sco" title="Scots language text">Norlin Airlann</i></i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">part</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> in the north-east of the island of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="#descriptions">variously described</a> as a country, province or region.<sup id="cite_ref-alphabeticalNI_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphabeticalNI-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interpretingNI-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-placeApart_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-placeApart-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Northern Ireland shares <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland%E2%80%93United_Kingdom_border" title="Republic of Ireland\xe2\x80\x93United Kingdom border">a border</a> to the south and west with the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>. In <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011</a>, its population was 1,810,863,<sup id="cite_ref-Norther_Ireland_Statistics_&amp;_Research_Agency_2012_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norther_Ireland_Statistics_&amp;_Research_Agency_2012-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> constituting about 30% of the island\'s total population and about 3% of the <a href="/wiki/Population_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Population of the United Kingdom">UK\'s population</a>. Established by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Act_1998" title="Northern Ireland Act 1998">Northern Ireland Act 1998</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> holds responsibility for a range of devolved policy matters, while other areas are reserved for the <a href="/wiki/British_government" class="mw-redirect" title="British government">British government</a>. Northern Ireland co-operates with the Republic of Ireland in some areas, and the Agreement granted the Republic the ability to "put forward views and proposals" with "determined efforts to resolve disagreements between the two governments".<sup id="cite_ref-gfa_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfa-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Northern Ireland was created in 1921, when <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Ireland was partitioned</a> between Northern Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%9322)" title="Southern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x9322)">Southern Ireland</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1920" title="Government of Ireland Act 1920">Government of Ireland Act 1920</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%9322)" title="Southern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x9322)">Southern Ireland</a>, which would become the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> in 1922, the majority of Northern Ireland\'s population were <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">unionists</a>, who wanted to remain within the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Most of these were the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Protestants" title="Ulster Protestants">Protestant</a> descendants of <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">colonists from Great Britain</a>. However, a significant minority, mostly <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Catholic">Catholics</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalism" title="Irish nationalism">nationalists</a> who wanted a <a href="/wiki/United_Ireland" title="United Ireland">united Ireland</a> independent of British rule.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Today, the former generally see themselves as British and the latter generally see themselves as Irish, while a distinct Northern Irish or <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> identity is claimed both by a large minority of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a> and by many of those who are non-aligned.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For most of the 20th century, when it came into existence, Northern Ireland was marked by discrimination and hostility between these two sides in what <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="First Minister of Northern Ireland">First Minister of Northern Ireland</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Trimble" title="David Trimble">David Trimble</a> called a "cold house" for Catholics. In the late 1960s, conflict between state forces and chiefly Protestant unionists on the one hand, and chiefly Catholic nationalists on the other, erupted into three decades of violence known as <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a>, which claimed over 3,500 lives and caused over 50,000 casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The 1998 Good Friday Agreement was a major step in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">peace process</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Decommissioning_of_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Decommissioning of weapons">decommissioning of weapons</a>, although <a href="/wiki/Sectarianism" title="Sectarianism">sectarianism</a> and religious segregation still remain major social problems, and sporadic violence has continued.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Northern Ireland has historically been the most industrialised region of Ireland. After declining as a result of the political and social turmoil of the Troubles,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Economy of Northern Ireland">its economy</a> has grown significantly since the late 1990s. The initial growth came from the "<a href="/wiki/Peace_Dividend#Other_uses" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace Dividend">peace dividend</a>" and the links which increased trade with the Republic of Ireland, continuing with a significant increase in tourism, investment and business from around the world. Unemployment in Northern Ireland peaked at 17.2% in 1986, dropping to 6.1% for June\xe2\x80\x93August 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Northern_Ireland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> and down by 1.2 percentage points over the year,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> similar to the UK figure of 6.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> 58.2% of those unemployed had been unemployed for over a year.\n</p><p>Prominent <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Culture of Northern Ireland">artists and sportspeople</a> from Northern Ireland include <a href="/wiki/Van_Morrison" title="Van Morrison">Van Morrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rory_McIlroy" title="Rory McIlroy">Rory McIlroy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joey_Dunlop" title="Joey Dunlop">Joey Dunlop</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wayne_McCullough" title="Wayne McCullough">Wayne McCullough</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Best" title="George Best">George Best</a>. Some people from Northern Ireland prefer to identify as Irish (e.g., poet <a href="/wiki/Seamus_Heaney" title="Seamus Heaney">Seamus Heaney</a> and actor <a href="/wiki/Liam_Neeson" title="Liam Neeson">Liam Neeson</a>) while others prefer to identify as British (e.g. actor <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Branagh" title="Kenneth Branagh">Kenneth Branagh</a>). Cultural links between Northern Ireland, the rest of Ireland, and the rest of the UK are complex, with Northern Ireland sharing both the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Ireland" title="Culture of Ireland">culture of Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">culture of the United Kingdom</a>. In many sports, the island of Ireland fields a single team, a notable exception being association football. Northern Ireland <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_at_the_Commonwealth_Games" title="Northern Ireland at the Commonwealth Games">competes separately</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>, and people from Northern Ireland may compete for either <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_at_the_Olympics" title="Great Britain at the Olympics">Great Britain</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ireland_at_the_Olympics" title="Ireland at the Olympics">Ireland</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ireland" title="History of Ireland">History of Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster#History" title="Ulster">Ulster \xc2\xa7&#160;History</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg/220px-Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg/330px-Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg/440px-Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3099" data-file-height="1953" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cannon_on_Derry_City_Walls_SMC_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cannon on <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Derry#City_walls" title="Derry">city walls</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The region that is now Northern Ireland was the bedrock of the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War_(Ireland)" title="Nine Years&#39; War (Ireland)">Irish war of resistance</a> against <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">programmes of colonialism</a> in the late 16th century. The English-controlled <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> had been declared by the English king <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> in 1542, but Irish resistance made English control fragmentary. Following Irish defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kinsale" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Kinsale">Battle of Kinsale</a>, though, the region\'s <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a>, Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> <a href="/wiki/Flight_of_the_Earls" title="Flight of the Earls">fled to continental Europe</a> in 1607 and the region became subject to major programmes of colonialism by Protestant English (mainly <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>) and Scottish (mainly <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a>) settlers. <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">A rebellion in 1641</a> by Irish aristocrats against English rule resulted in a massacre of settlers in Ulster in the context of a <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">war breaking out between England, Scotland and Ireland</a> fuelled by religious intolerance in government. Victories by English forces in that war and further Protestant victories in the <a href="/wiki/Williamite_War_in_Ireland" title="Williamite War in Ireland">Williamite War in Ireland</a> toward the close of the 17th century solidified <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Anglican</a> rule in Ireland. In Northern Ireland, the victories of the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Derry" title="Siege of Derry">Siege of Derry</a> (1689) and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Boyne" title="Battle of the Boyne">Battle of the Boyne</a> (1690) in this latter war are still celebrated by some Protestants (both Anglican and Presbyterian).<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG/220px-CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG/330px-CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG/440px-CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CIMG1495_ScraboHorizView.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scrabo_Tower" title="Scrabo Tower">Scrabo Tower</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">County Down</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Following the victory of 1691, and contrary to the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Limerick" title="Treaty of Limerick">Treaty of Limerick</a>, after the Pope who had been allied to William of Orange recognised James II as continuing king of Great Britain and Ireland in place of William, a series of <a href="/wiki/Penal_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal laws">penal laws</a> was passed by the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Anglican ruling class</a> in Ireland. Their intention was to materially disadvantage the Catholic community and, to a lesser extent, the Presbyterian community. In the context of open institutional discrimination, the 18th century saw <a href="/wiki/Secret_societies" class="mw-redirect" title="Secret societies">secret, militant societies</a> develop in communities in the region and act on <a href="/wiki/Sectarianism" title="Sectarianism">sectarian</a> tensions in violent attacks. These events escalated at the end of the century following an event known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Diamond" title="Battle of the Diamond">Battle of the Diamond</a>, which saw the supremacy of the Anglican and Presbyterian <a href="/wiki/Peep_o%27Day_Boys" class="mw-redirect" title="Peep o&#39;Day Boys">Peep o\'Day Boys</a> over the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Defenders_(Ireland)" title="Defenders (Ireland)">Defenders</a> and leading to the formation of the Anglican <a href="/wiki/Orange_Order" title="Orange Order">Orange Order</a>. <a href="/wiki/1798_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="1798 Rebellion">A rebellion in 1798</a> led by the cross-community Belfast-based <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_United_Irishmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Society of the United Irishmen">Society of the United Irishmen</a> and inspired by the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> sought to break the constitutional ties between Ireland and Britain and unite Irish people of all religions. Following this, in an attempt to quell sectarianism and force the removal of discriminatory laws (and to prevent the spread of French-style republicanism to Ireland), the government of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> pushed for the two kingdoms to be merged. The new state, formed in 1801, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>, was governed from a single government and parliament based in London.\n</p><p>Between 1717 and 1775 some 250,000 people from Ulster emigrated to the British North American colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> It is estimated that there are more than 27&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scotch-Irish Americans</a> now living in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition_of_Ireland">Partition of Ireland</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Partition of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/de/Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg/220px-Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/de/Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg/330px-Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/de/Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="312" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carson_signing_Solemn_League_and_Covenant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Signing of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Covenant" title="Ulster Covenant">Ulster Covenant</a> in 1912 in opposition to Home Rule</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">legal reforms</a> started in the late 18th century continued to remove statutory discrimination against Catholics, and progressive programmes enabled tenant farmers to buy land from landlords. By the close of the century, autonomy for Ireland within the United Kingdom, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement" title="Irish Home Rule movement">Home Rule</a></i>, was regarded as highly likely. In 1912, after decades of obstruction from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, Home Rule became a near-certainty. A clash between the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> and House of Lords over a controversial budget produced the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a>, which enabled the veto of the Lords to be overturned. The House of Lords veto had been the unionists\' main guarantee that Home Rule would not be enacted because the majority of members of the House of Lords were unionists. In response, opponents to Home Rule, from <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative and Unionist Party</a> leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> and Dublin-based <a href="/wiki/Barrister" title="Barrister">barrister</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carson" title="Edward Carson">Sir Edward Carson</a> to militant working class unionists in Ireland, threatened the use of violence. In 1914, <a href="/wiki/Larne_Gun_Running" class="mw-redirect" title="Larne Gun Running">they smuggled thousands of rifles and rounds of ammunition</a> from <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Imperial Germany</a> for use by the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteers" title="Ulster Volunteers">Ulster Volunteers</a> (UVF), a paramilitary organisation opposed to the implementation of Home Rule.\n</p><p>Unionists were in a minority in Ireland as a whole, but in the northern province of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> they were a very large majority in <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">County Down</a>, small majorities in <a href="/wiki/County_Armagh" title="County Armagh">County Armagh</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Londonderry" title="County Londonderry">County Londonderry</a> and a substantial minority in Ulster\'s five other counties.<sup id="cite_ref-StephenGwynn_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StephenGwynn-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> These four counties, as well as <a href="/wiki/County_Fermanagh" title="County Fermanagh">County Fermanagh</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Tyrone" title="County Tyrone">County Tyrone</a>, would later constitute Northern Ireland. Most of the remaining 26 counties which later became the Republic of Ireland were overwhelmingly majority-nationalist.\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_Crisis" title="Home Rule Crisis">Home Rule Crisis</a> the possibility was discussed of a "temporary" partition of these six counties from the rest of Ireland. In 1914, the <i><a href="/wiki/Third_Home_Rule_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Home Rule Bill">Third Home Rule Bill</a></i> received <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1914" title="Government of Ireland Act 1914">Government of Ireland Act 1914</a>. However, its implementation was suspended before it came into effect because of the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/WW_I" class="mw-redirect" title="WW I">First World War</a>, and the Amending Bill to partition Ireland was abandoned. The war was expected to last only a few weeks but in fact, lasted four years. By the end of the war (during which the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> had taken place), the Act was seen as unimplementable. Public opinion among nationalists had shifted during the war from a demand for home rule to one for full independence. In 1919, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> proposed a new bill be established by the cabinet\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Walter_Long,_1st_Viscount_Long#Political_career,_1911\xe2\x80\x931921" title="Walter Long, 1st Viscount Long">Walter Long</a> Committee</i> on Ireland, which by adopting findings of his (Lloyd George\'s) inconclusive 1917-18 <a href="/wiki/Irish_Convention#Home_Rule_after-life" title="Irish Convention">Irish Convention</a> would divide Ireland into two Home Rule areas: twenty-six counties being ruled from <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> and six being ruled from <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>. Straddling these two areas would be a shared <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lord Lieutenant of Ireland</a> who would appoint both governments and a <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ireland" title="Council of Ireland">Council of Ireland</a>, which Lloyd George believed would evolve into an all-Ireland parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Events overtook the government. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1918" title="United Kingdom general election, 1918">general election of 1918</a>, the pro-independence <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> won 73 of the 105 parliamentary seats in Ireland and unilaterally established the <a href="/wiki/First_D%C3%A1il" title="First D\xc3\xa1il">First D\xc3\xa1il</a>, an extrajudicial parliament in Ireland. <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">Ireland was partitioned</a> between Northern Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%9322)" title="Southern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x9322)">Southern Ireland</a> in 1921 under the terms of Lloyd George\'s <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1920" title="Government of Ireland Act 1920">Government of Ireland Act 1920</a><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> during the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish War">Anglo-Irish War</a> between Irish republican and British forces. The war ended on 6 December 1921, with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a>, which created the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>. Under the terms of the treaty, Northern Ireland would become part of the Free State unless the government opted out by presenting an address to the king, although in practice partition remained in place.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland">Northern Ireland</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Ireland" title="History of Northern Ireland">History of Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg/220px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg.png" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg/330px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg/440px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="698" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_Arms_of_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Coat of Arms of Northern Ireland">Coat of Arms of Northern Ireland</a> used between 1924 and 1973</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg/220px-Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg/330px-Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg/440px-Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="945" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_new_Northern_Ireland_Parliament_Buildings.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Opening of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Buildings_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Parliament Buildings (Northern Ireland)">Stormont</a> in 1932</div></div></div><p>\nAs expected, the Houses of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament of Northern Ireland</a> resolved on 7 December 1922 (the day after the establishment of the Irish Free State) to exercise its right to opt out of the Free State by making an address to the <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">King</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The text of the address was: </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Most Gracious Sovereign, We, your Majesty\'s most dutiful and loyal subjects, the Senators and Commons of Northern Ireland in Parliament assembled, having learnt of the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State_Constitution_Act_1922" title="Irish Free State Constitution Act 1922">Irish Free State Constitution Act 1922</a>, being the Act of Parliament for the ratification of the Articles of Agreement for a Treaty between Great Britain and Ireland, do, by this humble Address, pray your Majesty that the powers of the Parliament and Government of the Irish Free State shall no longer extend to Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p> Shortly afterwards, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Boundary_Commission" title="Irish Boundary Commission">Boundary Commission</a> was established to decide on the territorial boundaries between the Irish Free State and Northern Ireland. Owing to the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">civil war in the Free State</a>, the work of the commission was delayed until 1925. Leaders in Dublin expected a substantial reduction in the territory of Northern Ireland, with nationalist areas moving to the Free State. However, the commission\'s report recommended only that some small portions of land should be ceded from Northern Ireland to the Free State and even that a small amount of land should be ceded from the Free State to Northern Ireland. To prevent argument, this report was suppressed and, in exchange for a waiver to the Free State\'s obligations to the UK\'s public debt and the dissolution of the Council of Ireland (sought by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Government of Northern Ireland">Government of Northern Ireland</a>), the initial six-county border was maintained with no changes.\n</p><p>In June 1940, to encourage the neutral Irish state to join with the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a>, British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> indicated to the <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89amon_de_Valera" title="\xc3\x89amon de Valera">\xc3\x89amon de Valera</a> that the United Kingdom would push for Irish unity, but believing that Churchill could not deliver, de Valera declined the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The British did not inform the Government of Northern Ireland that they had made the offer to the Dublin government, and De Valera\'s rejection was not publicised until 1970.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ireland_Act_1949" title="Ireland Act 1949">Ireland Act 1949</a> gave the first legal guarantee that the region would not cease to be part of the United Kingdom without the consent of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament of Northern Ireland</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Troubles">The Troubles</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">The Troubles</a></div>\n<p>The Troubles, which started in the late 1960s, consisted of about thirty years of recurring acts of intense violence during which 3,254 people were killed<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> with over 50,000 casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> From 1969 to 2003 there were over 36,900 shooting incidents and over 16,200 bombings or attempted bombings associated with The Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The conflict was caused by the disputed status of Northern Ireland within the United Kingdom and the discrimination against the <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish nationalist">Irish nationalist</a> minority by the dominant <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">unionist</a> majority.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> From 1967 to 1972 the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association</a> (NICRA), which modelled itself on the US civil rights movement, led a campaign of <a href="/wiki/Civil_resistance" title="Civil resistance">civil resistance</a> to anti-Catholic discrimination in housing, employment, policing, and electoral procedures. The franchise for local government elections included only rate-payers and their spouses, and so excluded over a quarter of the electorate. While the majority of disenfranchised electors were Protestant, but Catholics were over-represented since they were poorer and had more adults still living in the family home.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png/440px-Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png" width="440" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png/660px-Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png/880px-Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png 2x" data-file-width="998" data-file-height="561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Troubles_deaths_by_perpetrator.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Responsibility for Troubles-related deaths between 1969 and 2001</div></div></div>\n<p>NICRA\'s campaign, seen by many unionists as an <a href="/wiki/Irish_republican" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish republican">Irish republican</a> front, and the violent reaction to it, proved to be a precursor to a more violent period.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> As early as 1969, armed campaigns of paramilitary groups began, including the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA_campaign_1969-1997" class="mw-redirect" title="Provisional IRA campaign 1969-1997">Provisional IRA campaign of 1969\xe2\x80\x931997</a> which was aimed at the end of British rule in Northern Ireland and the creation of a <a href="/wiki/United_Ireland" title="United Ireland">United Ireland</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force" title="Ulster Volunteer Force">Ulster Volunteer Force</a>, formed in 1966 in response to the perceived erosion of both the British character and unionist domination of Northern Ireland. The state security forces \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and the police (the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a>) \xe2\x80\x93 were also involved in the violence. The British government\'s position is that its forces were neutral in the conflict, trying to uphold law and order in Northern Ireland and the right of the people of Northern Ireland to democratic self-determination. Republicans regarded the state forces as <a href="/wiki/Combatants" class="mw-redirect" title="Combatants">combatants</a> in the conflict, pointing to the <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles#Collusion_between_security_forces_and_loyalist_paramilitaries" title="The Troubles">collusion between the state forces and the loyalist paramilitaries</a> as proof of this.  The "Ballast" investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Police_Ombudsman" class="mw-redirect" title="Police Ombudsman">Police Ombudsman</a> has confirmed that British forces, and in particular the RUC, did collude with <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Loyalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Loyalism">loyalist</a> paramilitaries, were involved in murder, and did obstruct the course of justice when such claims had been investigated,<sup id="cite_ref-Ballast_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ballast-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> although the extent to which such collusion occurred is still hotly disputed.\n</p><p>As a consequence of the worsening security situation, autonomous regional government for Northern Ireland was suspended in 1972. Alongside the violence, there was a political deadlock between the major political parties in Northern Ireland, including those who condemned violence, over the future status of Northern Ireland and the form of government there should be within Northern Ireland. In 1973, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_referendum,_1973" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland referendum, 1973">Northern Ireland held a referendum</a> to determine if it should remain in the United Kingdom, or be part of a united Ireland. The vote went heavily in favour (98.9%) of maintaining the status quo. Approximately 57.5% of the total electorate voted in support, but only 1% of Catholics voted following a boycott organised by the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_and_Labour_Party" title="Social Democratic and Labour Party">Social Democratic and Labour Party</a> (SDLP).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_process">Peace process</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">Northern Ireland peace process</a></div>\n<p>The Troubles were brought to an uneasy end by a <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">peace process</a> which included the declaration of ceasefires by most paramilitary organisations and the complete decommissioning of their weapons, the reform of the police, and the corresponding withdrawal of army troops from the streets and from sensitive border areas such as South Armagh and <a href="/wiki/Fermanagh" title="Fermanagh">Fermanagh</a>, as agreed by the signatories to the Belfast Agreement (commonly known as the "<a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>"). This reiterated the long-held British position, which had never before been fully acknowledged by successive Irish governments, that Northern Ireland will remain within the United Kingdom until a majority of voters in Northern Ireland decides otherwise. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">Constitution of Ireland</a> was amended in 1999 to remove a claim of the "Irish nation" to sovereignty over the entire island (in Article 2).<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg/220px-Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg/330px-Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg/440px-Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3479" data-file-height="2319" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_and_Northern_Ireland_Ministers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First Minister <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a> (DUP) center, his deputy <a href="/wiki/Martin_McGuinness" title="Martin McGuinness">Martin McGuinness</a> (Sinn F\xc3\xa9in) left, and Scottish First Minister <a href="/wiki/Alex_Salmond" title="Alex Salmond">Alex Salmond</a> right in 2008.</div></div></div>\n<p>The new <a href="/wiki/Articles_2_and_3_of_the_Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Articles 2 and 3 of the Constitution of Ireland">Articles 2 and 3</a>, added to the Constitution to replace the earlier articles, implicitly acknowledge that the status of Northern Ireland, and its relationships within the rest of the United Kingdom and with the Republic of Ireland, would only be changed with the agreement of a majority of voters in each jurisdiction. This aspect was also central to the Belfast Agreement which was signed in 1998 and ratified by referendums held simultaneously in both Northern Ireland and the Republic. At the same time, the British Government recognised for the first time, as part of the prospective, the so-called "Irish dimension": the principle that the people of the island of Ireland as a whole have the right, without any outside interference, to solve the issues between North and South by mutual consent.<sup id="cite_ref-Parliament_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliament-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The latter statement was key to winning support for the agreement from nationalists. It established a devolved power-sharing government within Northern Ireland, which must consist of both unionist and nationalist parties. These institutions were suspended by the <a href="/wiki/British_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="British Government">British Government</a> in 2002 after <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">Police Service of Northern Ireland</a> (PSNI) allegations of spying by people working for Sinn F\xc3\xa9in at the Assembly (<a href="/wiki/Stormontgate" title="Stormontgate">Stormontgate</a>). The resulting case against the accused Sinn F\xc3\xa9in member collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 July 2005, the Provisional IRA declared an end to its campaign and has since decommissioned what is thought to be all of its <a href="/wiki/Arsenal" title="Arsenal">arsenal</a>. This final act of decommissioning was performed in accordance with the Belfast Agreement of 1998 and under the watch of the <a href="/wiki/Independent_International_Commission_on_Decommissioning" title="Independent International Commission on Decommissioning">Independent International Commission on Decommissioning</a> and two external church witnesses. Many unionists, however, remain sceptical. The <a href="/wiki/Independent_International_Commission_on_Decommissioning" title="Independent International Commission on Decommissioning">International Commission</a> later confirmed that the main loyalist paramilitary groups, the UDA, UVF and the Red Hand Commando, had decommissioned what is thought to be all of their arsenals, witnessed by a <a href="/wiki/Robin_Eames" title="Robin Eames">former archbishop</a> and a former top civil servant.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Politicians elected to the Assembly at the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2003" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2003">2003 Assembly election</a> were called together on 15 May 2006 under the Northern Ireland Act 2006<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> for the purpose of electing a <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_and_deputy_First_Minister_of_Northern_Ireland" title="First Minister and deputy First Minister of Northern Ireland">First Minister and deputy First Minister of Northern Ireland</a> and choosing the members of an Executive (before 25 November 2006) as a preliminary step to the restoration of devolved government.\n</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2007" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2007">election</a> held on 7 March 2007, devolved government returned on 8 May 2007 with <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a> (DUP) leader <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a> and Sinn F\xc3\xa9in deputy leader <a href="/wiki/Martin_McGuinness" title="Martin McGuinness">Martin McGuinness</a> taking office as First Minister and deputy First Minister, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In its white paper on <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> the United Kingdom government reiterated its commitment to the Belfast Agreement. With regard to Northern Ireland\'s status, it said that the UK Government\'s "clearly-stated preference is to retain Northern Ireland\xe2\x80\x99s current constitutional position: as part of the UK, but with strong links to Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Politics of Northern Ireland">Politics of Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>\n<p>The main political divide in Northern Ireland is between unionists, who wish to see Northern Ireland continue as part of the United Kingdom, and nationalists, who wish to see Northern Ireland unified with the Republic of Ireland, independent from the United Kingdom.  These two opposing views are linked to deeper cultural divisions. Unionists are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Protestant">Ulster Protestant</a>, descendants of mainly <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a>, English, and <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> settlers as well as <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a> who converted to one of the Protestant denominations. Nationalists are overwhelmingly Catholic and descend from the population predating the settlement, with a minority from the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a> as well as some converts from Protestantism. Discrimination against nationalists under the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Buildings_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Parliament Buildings (Northern Ireland)">Stormont</a> government (1921\xe2\x80\x931972) gave rise to the civil rights movement in the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While some unionists argue that discrimination was not just due to religious or political bigotry, but also the result of more complex socio-economic, socio-political and geographical factors,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> its existence, and the manner in which nationalist anger at it was handled, were a major contributing factor to the Troubles. The political unrest went through its most violent phase between 1968 and 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, 36% of the population defined themselves as unionist, 24% as nationalist and 40% defined themselves as neither.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2015 opinion poll, 70% express a long-term preference of the maintenance of Northern Ireland\'s membership of the United Kingdom (either <a href="/wiki/Direct_rule_over_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct rule over Northern Ireland">directly ruled</a> or with <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved government</a>), while 14% express a preference for membership of a united Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> This discrepancy can be explained by the overwhelming preference among Protestants to remain a part of the UK (93%), while Catholic preferences are spread across a number of solutions to the constitutional question including remaining a part of the UK (47%), a united Ireland (32%), Northern Ireland becoming an independent state (4%), and those who "don\'t know" (16%).<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Official voting figures, which reflect views on the "national question" along with issues of candidate, geography, personal loyalty and historic voting patterns, show 54% of Northern Ireland voters vote for unionist parties, 42% vote for nationalist parties and 4% vote "other". Opinion polls consistently show that the election results are not necessarily an indication of the electorate\'s stance regarding the constitutional status of Northern Ireland. Most of the population of Northern Ireland are at least nominally Christian, mostly Roman Catholic and Protestant denominations. Many voters (regardless of religious affiliation) are attracted to unionism\'s <a href="/wiki/National_conservatism" title="National conservatism">conservative</a> policies, while other voters are instead attracted to the traditionally leftist Sinn F\xc3\xa9in and SDLP and their respective party platforms for <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2009_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2009-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the most part, Protestants feel a strong connection with Great Britain and wish for Northern Ireland to remain part of the United Kingdom. Many Catholics however, generally aspire to a United Ireland or are less certain about how to solve the constitutional question. In the 2015 survey by Northern Ireland Life and Times, 47% of Northern Irish Catholics supported Northern Ireland remaining a part of the United Kingdom, either by direct rule (6%) or devolved government (41%).\n</p><p>Protestants have a slight majority in Northern Ireland, according to the latest Northern Ireland Census. The make-up of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> reflects the appeals of the various parties within the population. Of the 108 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Legislative_Assembly_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Member of the Legislative Assembly (Northern Ireland)">Members of the Legislative Assembly (MLAs)</a>, 56 are unionists and 40 are nationalists (the remaining 12 are classified as "other").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Elections in Northern Ireland">Elections in Northern Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_law" title="Northern Ireland law">Northern Ireland law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StormontGeneral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/StormontGeneral.jpg/220px-StormontGeneral.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/StormontGeneral.jpg/330px-StormontGeneral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/StormontGeneral.jpg/440px-StormontGeneral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StormontGeneral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Parliament_Buildings_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Parliament Buildings (Northern Ireland)">Parliament Buildings</a> at <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Buildings_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Parliament Buildings (Northern Ireland)">Stormont</a>, Belfast, seat of the assembly.</div></div></div>\n<p>Since 1998, Northern Ireland has had <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> government within the United Kingdom, presided over by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> and a cross-community government (the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Executive" title="Northern Ireland Executive">Northern Ireland Executive</a>). The UK Government and UK Parliament are responsible for <a href="/wiki/Reserved_and_excepted_matters" title="Reserved and excepted matters">reserved and excepted matters</a>. Reserved matters comprise listed policy areas (such as <a href="/wiki/Civil_aviation" title="Civil aviation">civil aviation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" class="mw-redirect" title="Units of measurement">units of measurement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_genetics" title="Human genetics">human genetics</a>) that Parliament may devolve to the Assembly some time in the future. Excepted matters (such as <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a>, taxation and elections) are never expected to be considered for devolution. On all other governmental matters, the Executive together with the 90-member Assembly may legislate for and govern Northern Ireland. Devolution in Northern Ireland is dependent upon participation by members of the Northern Ireland executive in the <a href="/wiki/North/South_Ministerial_Council" title="North/South Ministerial Council">North/South Ministerial Council</a>, which coordinates areas of co-operation (such as agriculture, education and health) between Northern Ireland and the Republic of Ireland. Additionally, "in recognition of the Irish Government\'s special interest in Northern Ireland", the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland" title="Government of Ireland">Government of Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">Government of the United Kingdom</a> co-operate closely on non-devolved matters through the <a href="/wiki/British-Irish_Intergovernmental_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="British-Irish Intergovernmental Conference">British-Irish Intergovernmental Conference</a>.\n</p><p>Elections to the Northern Ireland Assembly are by <a href="/wiki/Single_transferable_vote" title="Single transferable vote">single transferable vote</a> with five Members of the Legislative Assembly (MLAs) elected from each of 18 <a href="/wiki/List_of_parliamentary_constituencies_in_Northern_Ireland" title="List of parliamentary constituencies in Northern Ireland">parliamentary constituencies</a>. In addition, eighteen representatives (Members of Parliament, MPs) are elected to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">lower house of the UK parliament</a>  from the same constituencies using the <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first-past-the-post</a> system. However, not all of those elected take their seats. Sinn F\xc3\xa9in MPs, currently seven, refuse to take the oath to serve the Queen that is required before MPs are allowed to take their seats. In addition, the upper house of the UK parliament, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, currently has some 25 appointed <a href="/wiki/List_of_Northern_Ireland_members_of_the_House_of_Lords" title="List of Northern Ireland members of the House of Lords">members from Northern Ireland</a>. Northern Ireland itself forms <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="Northern Ireland (European Parliament constituency)">a single constituency</a> for elections to the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Office" title="Northern Ireland Office">Northern Ireland Office</a> represents the UK government in Northern Ireland on reserved matters and represents Northern Ireland\'s interests within the UK Government. Additionally, the Republic\'s government also has the right to "put forward views and proposals" on non-devolved matters in relation to Northern Ireland. The Northern Ireland Office is led by the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a>, who sits in the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet of the United Kingdom</a>.\n</p><p>Northern Ireland is a distinct legal <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a>, separate from the two other jurisdictions in the United Kingdom (<a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">England and Wales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scots_law" title="Scots law">Scotland</a>). <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_law" title="Northern Ireland law">Northern Ireland law</a> developed from Irish law that existed before the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">partition of Ireland</a> in 1921. Northern Ireland is a <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> jurisdiction and its common law is similar to that in England and Wales. However, there are important differences in law and procedure between Northern Ireland and England and Wales. The body of <a href="/wiki/Statutory_law" title="Statutory law">statute law</a> affecting Northern Ireland reflects the history of Northern Ireland, including Acts of the Parliament of the United Kingdom, the Northern Ireland Assembly, the former <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament of Northern Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Parliament of Ireland</a>, along with some Acts of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="Parliament of Great Britain">Parliament of Great Britain</a> that were extended to Ireland under <a href="/wiki/Poynings%27_Law" title="Poynings&#39; Law">Poynings\' Law</a> between 1494 and 1782.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descriptions"><span id="descriptions">Descriptions</span></span></h3>\n<p>There is no generally accepted term to describe what Northern Ireland is: province, region, country or something else.<sup id="cite_ref-alphabeticalNI_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphabeticalNI-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interpretingNI-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-placeApart_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-placeApart-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The choice of term can be controversial and can reveal the writer\'s political preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interpretingNI-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> This has been noted as a problem by several writers on Northern Ireland, with no generally recommended solution.<sup id="cite_ref-alphabeticalNI_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphabeticalNI-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interpretingNI-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-placeApart_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-placeApart-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Owing in part to the way in which the United Kingdom, and Northern Ireland, came into being, there is no legally defined term to describe what Northern Ireland \'is\'. There is also no uniform or guiding way to refer to Northern Ireland amongst the agencies of the UK government. For example, the websites of the Office of the Prime Minister of the United Kingdom<sup id="cite_ref-Number10CountriesWithinACountry_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Number10CountriesWithinACountry-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/UK_Statistics_Authority" title="UK Statistics Authority">UK Statistics Authority</a> describe the United Kingdom as being made up of four countries, one of these being Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-UKStatisticsAuthorityGeographyOfTheUK_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKStatisticsAuthorityGeographyOfTheUK-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Other pages on the same websites refer to Northern Ireland specifically as a "province" as do publications of the UK Statistics Authority.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The website of the Northern Ireland Statistics and Research Agency also refers to Northern Ireland as being a province<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> as does the website of the Office of Public Sector Information<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and other agencies within Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-fortnight_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fortnight-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Publications of <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">HM Treasury</a><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and the Department of Finance and Personnel of the Northern Ireland Executive,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> on the other hand, describe Northern Ireland as being a "region of the UK". The UK\'s submission to the 2007 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_the_Standardization_of_Geographical_Names" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Conference on the Standardization of Geographical Names">United Nations Conference on the Standardization of Geographical Names</a> defines the UK as being made up of two countries (England and Scotland), one <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">principality</a> (Wales) and one province (Northern Ireland).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike England, Scotland and Wales, Northern Ireland has no history of being an independent country or of being a nation in its own right.<sup id="cite_ref-niPolitics_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niPolitics-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Some writers describe the United Kingdom as being made up of three countries and one province<sup id="cite_ref-economyOfMega_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economyOfMega-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> or point out the difficulties with calling Northern Ireland a country.<sup id="cite_ref-dictPolSci_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dictPolSci-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Authors writing specifically about Northern Ireland dismiss the idea that Northern Ireland is a "country" in general terms,<sup id="cite_ref-alphabeticalNI_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphabeticalNI-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-placeApart_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-placeApart-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-organisationalApproach_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-organisationalApproach-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-marketsAndMoral_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marketsAndMoral-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and draw contrasts in this respect with England, Scotland and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-promoKnowledge_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-promoKnowledge-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Even for the period covering the first 50 years of Northern Ireland\'s existence, the term <i>country</i> is considered inappropriate by some political scientists on the basis that many decisions were still made in London.<sup id="cite_ref-niPolitics_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niPolitics-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The absence of a distinct nation of Northern Ireland, separate within the island of Ireland, is also pointed out as being a problem with using the term<sup id="cite_ref-placeApart_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-placeApart-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-unionistPolitics_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unionistPolitics-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-americanRevival_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-americanRevival-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and is in contrast to England, Scotland, and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-theorizingNat_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theorizingNat-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many commentators prefer to use the term "province", although that is also not without problems. It can arouse irritation, particularly among nationalists, for whom the title province is properly reserved for the traditional province of Ulster, of which Northern Ireland comprises six out of nine counties.<sup id="cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interpretingNI-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dictPolSci_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dictPolSci-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The BBC style guide is to refer to Northern Ireland as a province, and use of the term is common in literature and newspaper reports on Northern Ireland and the United Kingdom. Some authors have described the meaning of this term as being equivocal: referring to Northern Ireland as being a province both of the United Kingdom and of the traditional country of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-unionistPolitics_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unionistPolitics-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>"Region" is used by several UK government agencies and the European Union. Some authors choose this word but note that it is "unsatisfactory".<sup id="cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-interpretingNI-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-placeApart_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-placeApart-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Northern Ireland can also be simply described as "part of the UK", including by UK government offices.<sup id="cite_ref-Number10CountriesWithinACountry_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Number10CountriesWithinACountry-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_names">Alternative names</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alternative_names_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Alternative names for Northern Ireland">Alternative names for Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<p>Many people inside and outside Northern Ireland use other names for Northern Ireland, depending on their point of view. Disagreement on names, and the reading of political symbolism into the use or non-use of a word, also attaches itself to some urban centres. The most notable example is whether Northern Ireland\'s second city should be called <a href="/wiki/Derry-Londonderry_name_dispute" class="mw-redirect" title="Derry-Londonderry name dispute">"Derry" or "Londonderry"</a>.\n</p><p>Choice of language and <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">nomenclature</a> in Northern Ireland often reveals the cultural, ethnic and religious identity of the speaker. The first <a href="/wiki/Deputy_First_Minister_of_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy First Minister of Northern Ireland">Deputy First Minister of Northern Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seamus_Mallon" title="Seamus Mallon">Seamus Mallon</a>, was criticised by unionist politicians for calling the region the "North of Ireland" while Sinn F\xc3\xa9in has been criticised in a Dublin newspaper for referring to the "Six Counties".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Those who do not belong to any group but lean towards one side often tend to use the language of that group. Supporters of unionism in the British media (notably <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i>) regularly call Northern Ireland "Ulster".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Some media outlets in the Republic use "North of Ireland", "the North", or (less often) the "Six Counties".\n</p><p>Government and cultural organisations in Northern Ireland often use the word "Ulster" in their title; for example, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ulster" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Ulster">University of Ulster</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Museum" title="Ulster Museum">Ulster Museum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Orchestra" title="Ulster Orchestra">Ulster Orchestra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Ulster" title="BBC Radio Ulster">BBC Radio Ulster</a>.\n</p><p>Although some news bulletins since the 1990s have opted to avoid all contentious terms and use the official name, Northern Ireland, the term "the North" remains commonly used by broadcast media in the Republic.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Unionist_mural,_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Unionist_mural%2C_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg/170px-Unionist_mural%2C_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Unionist_mural%2C_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg/255px-Unionist_mural%2C_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Unionist_mural%2C_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg/340px-Unionist_mural%2C_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1936" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Unionist_mural,_Shankill_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unionist mural in Belfast</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unionist">Unionist</span></h4>\n<ul><li><b>Ulster</b>, strictly speaking, refers to the province of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, of which six of nine historical counties are in Northern Ireland. The term "Ulster" is widely used by unionists and the British press as shorthand for Northern Ireland, and is also favoured by <a href="/wiki/Ulster_nationalism" title="Ulster nationalism">Ulster nationalists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In the past, calls have been made for Northern Ireland\'s name to be changed to Ulster. This proposal was formally considered by the Government of Northern Ireland in 1937 and by the UK Government in 1949 but no change was made.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>The Province</b> refers to the historic Irish province of Ulster but today is used by some as shorthand for Northern Ireland. The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>, in its editorial guidance for <i>Reporting the United Kingdom</i>, states that "the Province" is an appropriate secondary synonym for Northern Ireland, while "Ulster" is not. It also suggests that "people of Northern Ireland" is preferred to "British" or "Irish", and the term "mainland" should be avoided in reference to Great Britain in relation to Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalist">Nationalist</span></h4>\n<ul><li><b>North of Ireland</b> \xe2\x80\x93 used to avoid using the name given by the British-enacted Government of Ireland Act 1920.</li>\n<li><b>The Six Counties</b> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">na S\xc3\xa9 Chontae</i>) \xe2\x80\x93 the Republic of Ireland is similarly described as the Twenty-Six Counties.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Some of the
