href="#cite_note-BelfastMetro-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>62,650</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#f0f0f0">7</td>\n<td align="left"><b><a href="/wiki/Ballymena" title="Ballymena">Ballymena</a></b></td>\n<td>30,590\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#f0f0f0">8</td>\n<td align="left"><b><a href="/wiki/Newtownards" title="Newtownards">Newtownards</a></b></td>\n<td>28,860\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#f0f0f0">9</td>\n<td align="left"><b><a href="/wiki/Newry" title="Newry">Newry</a></b></td>\n<td>28,080</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#f0f0f0">10</td>\n<td align="left"><b><a href="/wiki/Carrickfergus_(borough)" class="mw-redirect" title="Carrickfergus (borough)">Carrickfergus</a> </b><sup id="cite_ref-BelfastMetro_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BelfastMetro-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td>27,640</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citizenship_and_identity">Citizenship and identity</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British nationality law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationality_law" title="Irish nationality law">Irish nationality law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Most_common_nationality_2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Most_common_nationality_2011.png/220px-Most_common_nationality_2011.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Most_common_nationality_2011.png/330px-Most_common_nationality_2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Most_common_nationality_2011.png/440px-Most_common_nationality_2011.png 2x" data-file-width="18996" data-file-height="14756" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Most_common_nationality_2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of predominant national identity in the 2011 census</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 2011 census in Northern Ireland respondents gave their national identity as follows.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:right">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2">National<br />identity\n</th>\n<th rowspan="2">All\n</th>\n<th colspan="4">Religious affiliation\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="font-size:85%">\n<th>Catholic</th>\n<th>Protestant<br />and other<br />Christian</th>\n<th>Non-<br />Christian</th>\n<th>None\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">British</td>\n<td>48.4%</td>\n<td>12.9%</td>\n<td>81.6%</td>\n<td>50.1%</td>\n<td>55.9%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">Irish</td>\n<td>28.4%</td>\n<td>57.2%</td>\n<td>3.9%</td>\n<td>12.4%</td>\n<td>14.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">Northern Irish</td>\n<td>29.4%</td>\n<td>30.7%</td>\n<td>26.9%</td>\n<td>18.0%</td>\n<td>35.2%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">English, Scottish, or Welsh</td>\n<td>1.6%</td>\n<td>0.8%</td>\n<td>1.5%</td>\n<td>2.9%</td>\n<td>5.2%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">All other</td>\n<td>3.4%</td>\n<td>4.4%</td>\n<td>1.0%</td>\n<td>29.1%</td>\n<td>7.1%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Several studies and surveys carried out between 1971 and 2006 have indicated that, in general, most Protestants in Northern Ireland see themselves primarily as British, whereas a majority of Roman Catholics regard themselves primarily as Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> This does not, however, account for the <a href="/wiki/People_of_Northern_Ireland" title="People of Northern Ireland">complex identities within Northern Ireland</a>, given that many of the population regard themselves as "Ulster" or "Northern Irish", either as a primary or secondary identity. Overall, the Catholic population is somewhat more ethnically diverse than the more homogeneous Protestant population. 83.1% of Protestants identified as "British" or with a British ethnic group (English, Scottish, or Welsh) in the 2011 Census, whereas only 3.9% identified as "Irish". Meanwhile, 13.7% of Catholics identified as "British" or with a British ethnic group. A further 4.4% identified as "all other", which are largely immigrants, for example from <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>.\n</p><p>A 2008 survey found that 57% of Protestants described themselves as British, while 32% identified as Northern Irish, 6% as Ulster and 4% as Irish. Compared to a similar survey carried out in 1998, this shows a fall in the percentage of Protestants identifying as British and Ulster and a rise in those identifying as Northern Irish. The 2008 survey found that 61% of Catholics described themselves as Irish, with 25% identifying as Northern Irish, 8% as British and 1% as Ulster. These figures were largely unchanged from the 1998 results.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>People born in Northern Ireland are, with some exceptions, deemed by UK law to be <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">citizens of the United Kingdom</a>. They are also, with similar exceptions, entitled to be <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationality_law" title="Irish nationality law">citizens of Ireland</a>. This entitlement was reaffirmed in the 1998 <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> between the British and Irish governments, which provides that:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>...it is the birthright of all the people of Northern Ireland to identify themselves and be accepted as Irish or British, or both, as they may so choose, and accordingly [the two governments] confirm that their right to hold both British and Irish citizenship is accepted by both Governments and would not be affected by any future change in the status of Northern Ireland.</p></blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png/220px-Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png/330px-Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png/440px-Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png 2x" data-file-width="18996" data-file-height="14790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Predominant_passport_held_northern_ireland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of most commonly held passport</div></div></div>\n<p>As a result of the Agreement, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">Constitution of the Republic of Ireland</a> was amended. The current wording provides that people born in Northern Ireland are entitled to be Irish citizens on the same basis as people from any other part of the island.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neither government, however, extends its citizenship to all persons born in Northern Ireland. Both governments exclude some people born in Northern Ireland, in particular persons born without one parent who is a British or Irish citizen. The Irish restriction was given effect by the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-seventh_Amendment_of_the_Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Twenty-seventh Amendment of the Constitution of Ireland">twenty-seventh amendment to the Irish Constitution</a> in 2004. The position in UK nationality law is that most of those born in Northern Ireland are UK nationals, whether or not they so choose. Renunciation of British citizenship requires the payment of a fee, currently \xc2\xa3372.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 census</a> in Northern Ireland respondents stated that they held the following passports.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align:right">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2">Passport\n</th>\n<th rowspan="2">All usual<br />residents\n</th>\n<th colspan="4">Religion or religion brought up in\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Catholic</th>\n<th>Protestant<br />and other<br />Christian</th>\n<th>Other<br />Religions</th>\n<th>None\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">No passport</td>\n<td>18.9%</td>\n<td>19.2%</td>\n<td>18.5%</td>\n<td>9.9%</td>\n<td>20.2%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">United Kingdom</td>\n<td>59.1%</td>\n<td>38.4%</td>\n<td>77.8%</td>\n<td>56.0%</td>\n<td>65.2%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">Ireland</td>\n<td>20.8%</td>\n<td>40.5%</td>\n<td>4.1%</td>\n<td>10.0%</td>\n<td>7.2%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left">Other</td>\n<td>3.4%</td>\n<td>4.5%</td>\n<td>1.1%</td>\n<td>29.2%</td>\n<td>9.4%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Languages of Northern Ireland">Languages of Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png/220px-English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png/330px-English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png/440px-English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="833" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_dialects_in_Ulster_contrast.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approximate boundaries of the current and historical English/<a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> dialects in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>. South to north, the colour bands represent <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-English" title="Hiberno-English">Hiberno-English</a>, South-<a href="/wiki/Ulster_English" title="Ulster English">Ulster English</a>, Mid-Ulster English and the three traditional <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster Scots</a> areas. The Irish-speaking <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht" title="Gaeltacht">Gaeltacht</a> is not shown.</div></div></div>\n<p>English is spoken as a first language by almost all of the Northern Ireland population. It is the <i>de facto</i> official language and the <a href="/wiki/Administration_of_Justice_(Language)_Act_(Ireland)_1737" title="Administration of Justice (Language) Act (Ireland) 1737">Administration of Justice (Language) Act (Ireland) 1737</a> prohibits the use of languages other than English in legal proceedings.\n</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Belfast Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>, Irish and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster Scots</a> (an Ulster dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a>, sometimes known as <i>Ullans</i>), are recognised as "part of the cultural wealth of Northern Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Two all-island bodies for the promotion of these were created under the Agreement: <i><a href="/wiki/Foras_na_Gaeilge" title="Foras na Gaeilge">Foras na Gaeilge</a></i>, which promotes the Irish language, and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Scots Agency">Ulster Scots Agency</a>, which promotes the Ulster Scots dialect and culture. These operate separately under the aegis of <a href="/wiki/The_North/South_Language_Body" title="The North/South Language Body">the North/South Language Body</a>, which reports to the <a href="/wiki/North/South_Ministerial_Council" title="North/South Ministerial Council">North/South Ministerial Council</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/British_government" class="mw-redirect" title="British government">British government</a> in 2001 ratified the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>. Irish (in Northern Ireland) was specified under Part III of the Charter, with a range of specific undertakings in relation to education, translation of statutes, interaction with public authorities, the use of placenames, media access, support for cultural activities and other matters. A lower level of recognition was accorded to Ulster Scots, under Part II of the Charter.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="English">English</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mid-Ulster_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid-Ulster English">Mid-Ulster English</a></div>\n<p>The dialect of English spoken in Northern Ireland shows influence from the lowland <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> There are supposedly some minute differences in pronunciation between Protestants and Catholics, the best known of which is the name of the letter <i>h</i>, which Protestants tend to pronounce as "aitch", as in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>, and Catholics tend to pronounce as "haitch", as in <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-English" title="Hiberno-English">Hiberno-English</a>. However, geography is a much more important determinant of dialect than religious background.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish">Irish</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png/220px-Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png/330px-Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png/440px-Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png 2x" data-file-width="18870" data-file-height="14693" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_those_with_some_ability_in_Irish_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of people aged 3+ claiming to have some ability in Irish in the 2011 census</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Irish language in Northern Ireland">Irish language in Northern Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Irish" title="Ulster Irish">Ulster Irish</a></div>\n<p>The Irish language (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>: <i lang="ga">an Ghaeilge</i>), or <i>Gaelic</i>, is a native language of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> It was spoken predominantly throughout what is now Northern Ireland before the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Plantations" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Plantations">Ulster Plantations</a> in the 17th century and most place names in Northern Ireland are <a href="/wiki/Anglicised" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglicised">anglicised</a> versions of a Gaelic name. Today, the language is often associated with Irish nationalism (and thus with Catholics). However, in the 19th century, the language was seen as a common heritage, with Ulster Protestants playing a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_revival" title="Gaelic revival">Gaelic revival</a>.\n</p><p>In the 2011 census, 11% of the population of Northern Ireland claimed "some knowledge of Irish"<sup id="cite_ref-Census_2011_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_2011-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and 3.7% reported being able to "speak, read, write and understand" Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-Census_2011_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_2011-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In another survey, from 1999, 1% of respondents said they spoke it as their main language at home.<sup id="cite_ref-Survey-NorthernIreland_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Survey-NorthernIreland-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The dialect spoken in Northern Ireland, Ulster Irish, has two main types, East Ulster Irish and Donegal Irish (or West Ulster Irish),<sup id="cite_ref-pipex1_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pipex1-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> is the one closest to <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> (which developed into a separate language from Irish Gaelic in the 17th century). Some words and phrases are shared with Scots Gaelic, and the dialects of east Ulster \xe2\x80\x93 those of <a href="/wiki/Rathlin_Island" title="Rathlin Island">Rathlin Island</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Glens_of_Antrim" title="Glens of Antrim">Glens of Antrim</a> \xe2\x80\x93 were very similar to the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a>, the part of Scotland nearest to Ireland. And those dialects of Armagh and Down were also very similar to the dialects of Galloway.\n</p><p>Use of the Irish language in Northern Ireland today is politically sensitive. The erection by some <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Local government in Northern Ireland">district councils</a> of bilingual street names in both English and Irish,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> invariably in predominantly nationalist districts, is resisted by unionists who claim that it creates a "chill factor" and thus harms community relationships. Efforts by members of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> to legislate for some official uses of the language have failed to achieve the required cross-community support, and the UK government has declined to legislate. There has recently been an increase in interest in the language among unionists in East Belfast.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ulster_Scots">Ulster Scots</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png/220px-Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png/330px-Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png/440px-Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="19184" data-file-height="14938" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_showing_the_percentage_of_people_aged_3%2B_claiming_to_have_some_ability_in_Ulster_Scots_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of people aged 3+ claiming to have some ability in Ulster Scots in the 2011 census</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster Scots dialects</a></div>\n<p>Ulster Scots comprises varieties of the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a> spoken in Northern Ireland. For a native English speaker, "[Ulster Scots] is comparatively accessible, and even at its most intense can be understood fairly easily with the help of a glossary."<sup id="cite_ref-Poilin-BBC_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poilin-BBC-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Along with the Irish language, the Good Friday Agreement recognised the dialect as part of Northern Ireland\'s unique culture and the <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews_Agreement" title="St Andrews Agreement">St Andrews Agreement</a> recognised the need to "enhance and develop the Ulster Scots language, heritage and culture".<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Approximately 2% of the population claim to speak Ulster Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> However, the number speaking it as their main language in their home is negligible,<sup id="cite_ref-Survey-NorthernIreland_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Survey-NorthernIreland-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> with only 0.9% of 2011 census respondents claiming to be able to speak, read, write and understand Ulster-Scots. 8.1% professed to have "some ability" however.<sup id="cite_ref-Census_2011_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_2011-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sign_languages">Sign languages</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Sign_Language" title="Northern Ireland Sign Language">Northern Ireland Sign Language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sign_Language" title="Irish Sign Language">Irish Sign Language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/British_Sign_Language" title="British Sign Language">British Sign Language</a></div>\n<p>The most common <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign language</a> in Northern Ireland is <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Sign_Language" title="Northern Ireland Sign Language">Northern Ireland Sign Language</a> (NISL). However, because in the past Catholic families tended to send their deaf children to schools in Dublin<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> where <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sign_Language" title="Irish Sign Language">Irish Sign Language</a> (ISL) is commonly used, ISL is still common among many older deaf people from Catholic families.\n</p><p>Irish Sign Language (ISL) has some influence from the French family of sign language, which includes <a href="/wiki/American_Sign_Language" title="American Sign Language">American Sign Language</a> (ASL). NISL takes a large component from the British family of sign language (which also includes <a href="/wiki/Auslan" title="Auslan">Auslan</a>) with many borrowings from ASL. It is described as being related to Irish Sign Language at the <a href="/wiki/Syntactic" class="mw-redirect" title="Syntactic">syntactic</a> level while much of the <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a> is based on <a href="/wiki/British_Sign_Language" title="British Sign Language">British Sign Language</a> (BSL)<sup id="cite_ref-Janzen_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janzen-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> and American Sign Language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>As of  March&#160;2004<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Northern_Ireland&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/British_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="British Government">British Government</a> recognises only British Sign Language and Irish Sign Language as the official sign languages used in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-dcal_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dcal-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Culture of Northern Ireland">Culture of Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade,_Belfast,_September_2012_(010).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade%2C_Belfast%2C_September_2012_%28010%29.JPG/220px-Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade%2C_Belfast%2C_September_2012_%28010%29.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade%2C_Belfast%2C_September_2012_%28010%29.JPG/330px-Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade%2C_Belfast%2C_September_2012_%28010%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade%2C_Belfast%2C_September_2012_%28010%29.JPG/440px-Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade%2C_Belfast%2C_September_2012_%28010%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster_Covenant_Commemoration_Parade,_Belfast,_September_2012_(010).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Orange_Order" title="Orange Order">Orange</a> march</div></div></div>\n<p>Northern Ireland shares both the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Ireland" title="Culture of Ireland">culture of Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Culture of the United Kingdom">culture of the United Kingdom</a>. Those of Catholic background tend to identity more with Irish culture, and those of Protestant background more with British culture. This has caused the two communities to become <a href="/wiki/Pillarisation#Northern_Ireland:_institutions_by_pillar" title="Pillarisation">pillarised</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Parades_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Parades in Northern Ireland">Parades are a prominent feature</a> of Northern Ireland society,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> more so than in the rest of Ireland or in Britain. Most are held by Protestant fraternities such as the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Order" title="Orange Order">Orange Order</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_loyalism" title="Ulster loyalism">Ulster loyalist</a> marching bands. Each summer, during the "marching season", these groups have hundreds of parades, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_flags_issue" title="Northern Ireland flags issue">deck streets with British flags</a>, bunting and specially-made arches, and light <a href="/wiki/Eleventh_Night" title="Eleventh Night">large towering bonfires</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bryan130_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryan130-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The biggest parades are held on 12 July (<a href="/wiki/The_Twelfth" title="The Twelfth">The Twelfth</a>). There is often tension when these activities take place near Catholic neighbourhoods, which sometimes leads to violence.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the end of the Troubles, Northern Ireland has witnessed rising numbers of tourists. Attractions include cultural festivals, musical and artistic traditions, countryside and geographical sites of interest, <a href="/wiki/Public_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Public house">public houses</a>, welcoming hospitality and sports (especially golf and fishing). Since 1987 <a href="/wiki/Public_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Public house">public houses</a> have been allowed to open on Sundays, despite some opposition.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Cycle" title="Ulster Cycle">Ulster Cycle</a> is a large body of prose and verse centring on the traditional heroes of the <a href="/wiki/Ulaid" title="Ulaid">Ulaid</a> in what is now eastern Ulster. This is one of the four major cycles of <a href="/wiki/Irish_mythology" title="Irish mythology">Irish mythology</a>. The cycle centres on the reign of <a href="/wiki/Conchobar_mac_Nessa" title="Conchobar mac Nessa">Conchobar mac Nessa</a>, who is said to have been king of Ulster around the 1st century. He ruled from <a href="/wiki/Emain_Macha" class="mw-redirect" title="Emain Macha">Emain Macha</a> (now Navan Fort near Armagh), and had a fierce rivalry with queen <a href="/wiki/Medb" title="Medb">Medb</a> and king Ailill of Connacht and their ally, <a href="/wiki/Fergus_mac_R%C3%B3ich" title="Fergus mac R\xc3\xb3ich">Fergus mac R\xc3\xb3ich</a>, former king of Ulster. The foremost hero of the cycle is Conchobar\'s nephew <a href="/wiki/C%C3%BAchulainn" class="mw-redirect" title="C\xc3\xbachulainn">C\xc3\xbachulainn</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbols">Symbols</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_flags_issue" title="Northern Ireland flags issue">Northern Ireland flags issue</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flax_flowers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Flax_flowers.jpg/220px-Flax_flowers.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Flax_flowers.jpg/330px-Flax_flowers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Flax_flowers.jpg/440px-Flax_flowers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flax_flowers.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The logo for the Northern Ireland assembly is based on the flower of the <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> plant.<sup id="cite_ref-NI_logo_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NI_logo-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountain_(04).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fountain_%2804%29.JPG/220px-Fountain_%2804%29.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fountain_%2804%29.JPG/330px-Fountain_%2804%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Fountain_%2804%29.JPG/440px-Fountain_%2804%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4099" data-file-height="2732" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountain_(04).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The unionist <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Banner" title="Ulster Banner">Ulster Banner</a> on the foreground, with a republican <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ireland" title="Flag of Ireland">Irish flag</a> in the background on the left, in <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Northern Ireland comprises a patchwork of communities whose national loyalties are represented in some areas by flags flown from flagpoles or lamp posts. The <a href="/wiki/Union_Jack" title="Union Jack">Union Jack</a> and the former <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Flag of Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland flag</a> are flown in many loyalist areas, and the Tricolour, adopted by republicans as the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ireland" title="Flag of Ireland">flag of Ireland</a> in 1916,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> is flown in some republican areas. Even <a href="/wiki/Curb_(road)" class="mw-redirect" title="Curb (road)">kerbstones</a> in some areas are painted red-white-blue or green-white-orange, depending on whether local people express unionist/loyalist or nationalist/republican sympathies.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The official flag is that of the state having sovereignty over the territory, i.e. the Union Flag.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The former Northern Ireland flag, also known as the "<a href="/wiki/Ulster_Banner" title="Ulster Banner">Ulster Banner</a>" or "Red Hand Flag", is a banner derived from the coat of arms of the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Government of Northern Ireland">Government of Northern Ireland</a> until 1972. Since 1972, it has had no official status. The Union Flag and the Ulster Banner are used exclusively by unionists. UK flags policy states that in Northern Ireland, "The Ulster flag and the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Saltire" title="Saint Patrick&#39;s Saltire">Cross of St Patrick</a> have no official status and, under the Flags Regulations, are not permitted to be flown from Government Buildings."<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rugby_Football_Union" title="Irish Rugby Football Union">Irish Rugby Football Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> have used the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Saltire" title="Saint Patrick&#39;s Saltire">Saint Patrick\'s Saltire</a> or "Cross of St&#160;Patrick". This red <a href="/wiki/Saltire" title="Saltire">saltire</a> on a white field was used to represent Ireland in the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Flag of the United Kingdom">flag of the United Kingdom</a>. It is still used by some <a href="/wiki/British_army" class="mw-redirect" title="British army">British army</a> regiments. Foreign flags are also found, such as the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestinian</a> flags in some nationalist areas and Israeli flags in some unionist areas.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The United Kingdom <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of "<a href="/wiki/God_Save_the_Queen" title="God Save the Queen">God Save the Queen</a>" is often played at state events in Northern Ireland. At the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a> and some other sporting events, the Northern Ireland team uses the Ulster Banner as its flag\xe2\x80\x94notwithstanding its lack of official status\xe2\x80\x94and the <i><a href="/wiki/Londonderry_Air" title="Londonderry Air">Londonderry Air</a></i> (usually set to <a href="/wiki/Lyrics" title="Lyrics">lyrics</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Danny_Boy" title="Danny Boy">Danny Boy</a></i>), which also has no official status, as its <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_national_football_team" title="Northern Ireland national football team">national football team</a> also uses the Ulster Banner as its flag but uses "God Save The Queen" as its anthem.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\nMajor <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Athletic_Association" title="Gaelic Athletic Association">Gaelic Athletic Association</a> matches are opened by the national anthem of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Amhr%C3%A1n_na_bhFiann" title="Amhr\xc3\xa1n na bhFiann">Amhr\xc3\xa1n na bhFiann</a> (The Soldier\'s Song)", which is also used by most other all-Ireland sporting organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\nSince 1995, the <a href="/wiki/Ireland_rugby_union_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland rugby union team">Ireland rugby union team</a> has used a specially commissioned song, "<a href="/wiki/Ireland%27s_Call" title="Ireland&#39;s Call">Ireland\'s Call</a>" as the team\'s anthem. The Irish national anthem is also played at Dublin home matches, being the anthem of the host country.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Northern_Irish_murals" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Irish murals">Northern Irish murals</a> have become well-known features of Northern Ireland, depicting past and present events and documenting peace and cultural diversity. Almost 2,000 murals have been documented in Northern Ireland since the 1970s.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Sport in Northern Ireland">Sport in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<p>In Northern Ireland, sport is popular and important in the lives of many people. Sports tend to be organised on an <a href="/wiki/All-Ireland" title="All-Ireland">all-Ireland</a> basis, with a single team for the whole island.<sup id="cite_ref-theherald1_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theherald1-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The most notable exception is association football, which has separate governing bodies for each jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-theherald1_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theherald1-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Field_sports">Field sports</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Association_football">Association football</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_best_1976.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/George_best_1976.jpg/170px-George_best_1976.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/George_best_1976.jpg/255px-George_best_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/George_best_1976.jpg/340px-George_best_1976.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1430" data-file-height="1906" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_best_1976.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Best" title="George Best">George Best</a>, <a href="/wiki/People_of_Northern_Ireland" title="People of Northern Ireland">Northern Irish</a> international footballer and 1968 <a href="/wiki/Ballon_d%27Or" title="Ballon d&#39;Or">Ballon d\'Or</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_Football_Association" title="Irish Football Association">Irish Football Association</a> (IFA) serves as the organising body for association football in Northern Ireland, with the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Football_League" title="Northern Ireland Football League">Northern Ireland Football League</a> (NIFL) responsible for the independent administration of the three divisions of national domestic football, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Football_League_Cup" title="Northern Ireland Football League Cup">Northern Ireland Football League Cup</a>.\n</p><p>The highest level of competition within Northern Ireland are the <a href="/wiki/NIFL_Premiership" title="NIFL Premiership">NIFL Premiership</a> and the <a href="/wiki/NIFL_Championship" title="NIFL Championship">NIFL Championship</a>. However, many players from Northern Ireland compete with clubs in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>.\n</p><p>NIFL clubs are <a href="/wiki/Semi-professional" title="Semi-professional">semi-professional</a> or Intermediate.<a href="/wiki/NIFL_Premiership" title="NIFL Premiership">NIFL Premiership</a> clubs are  also eligible to compete in the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">UEFA Champions League</a> and <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Europa_League" title="UEFA Europa League">UEFA Europa League</a> with the league champions entering the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Champions_League" title="UEFA Champions League">Champions league</a> second qualifying round and the 2nd placed league finisher, the European play-off winners and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Cup" title="Irish Cup">Irish Cup</a> winners entering the <a href="/wiki/Europa_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Europa League">Europa League</a> second qualifying round.No clubs have ever reached the group stage.\n</p><p>Despite Northern Ireland\'s small population, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_national_football_team" title="Northern Ireland national football team">national team</a> qualified for the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">World Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/1958_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1958 FIFA World Cup">1958</a>, <a href="/wiki/1982_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1982 FIFA World Cup">1982</a> and <a href="/wiki/1986_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1986 FIFA World Cup">1986</a>, making it to the quarter-finals in 1958 and 1982 and made it the first knockout round in the <a href="/wiki/European_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="European Championships">European Championships</a> in 2016.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rugby_union">Rugby union</span></h4>\n<p>The six counties of Northern Ireland are among the nine governed by the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Rugby" title="Ulster Rugby">Ulster branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rugby_Football_Union" title="Irish Rugby Football Union">Irish Rugby Football Union</a>, the governing body of <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby union</a> in Ireland.  Ulster is one of the four professional provincial teams in Ireland and competes in the <a href="/wiki/Pro14" title="Pro14">Pro14</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heineken_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="Heineken Cup">European Cup</a>. It won the European Cup in 1999.\n</p><p>In international competitions, the <a href="/wiki/Ireland_national_rugby_union_team" title="Ireland national rugby union team">Ireland national rugby union team</a>\'s recent successes include four <a href="/wiki/Triple_Crown_(rugby_union)" title="Triple Crown (rugby union)">Triple Crowns</a> between 2004 and 2009 and a <a href="/wiki/Grand_Slam_(rugby_union)" title="Grand Slam (rugby union)">Grand Slam</a> in 2009 in the <a href="/wiki/Six_Nations_Championship" title="Six Nations Championship">Six Nations Championship</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cricket">Cricket</span></h4>\n<p>Northern Ireland plays as the <a href="/wiki/Ireland_cricket_team" title="Ireland cricket team">Ireland cricket team</a> which represents both Northern Ireland and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>. The Ireland Cricket team is a full member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">International Cricket Council</a>, having been granted <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test status</a> and full membership (along with <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghanistan cricket team">Afghanistan</a>) by the ICC in June 2017. They are currently able to compete in Test cricket, the highest level of competitive cricket in the international arena and they are one of the twelve full-member countries under the ICC.\n</p><p>\nIreland is the current champion of the <a href="/wiki/ICC_Intercontinental_Cup" title="ICC Intercontinental Cup">ICC Intercontinental Cup</a>. One of Ireland\'s regular international venues is <a href="/wiki/Stormont_(cricket_ground)" title="Stormont (cricket ground)">Stormont</a> in Belfast.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg/150px-Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg" width="150" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg/225px-Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg/300px-Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Canavan_-_SFC_2005_cc_3.0.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Peter_Canavan" title="Peter Canavan">Peter Canavan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyrone_GAA" title="Tyrone GAA">Tyrone</a> captain 2003</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic_games">Gaelic games</span></h4>\n<p>Gaelic games include <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_football" title="Gaelic football">Gaelic football</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurling" title="Hurling">hurling</a> (and <a href="/wiki/Camogie" title="Camogie">camogie</a>), <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_handball" title="Gaelic handball">handball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rounders" title="Rounders">rounders</a>. Of the four, football is the most popular in Northern Ireland.  Players play for local clubs with the best being selected for their county teams. The <a href="/wiki/Ulster_GAA" title="Ulster GAA">Ulster GAA</a> is the branch of the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Athletic_Association" title="Gaelic Athletic Association">Gaelic Athletic Association</a> that is responsible for the nine counties of Ulster, which include the six of Northern Ireland.\n</p><p>These nine county teams participate in the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Senior_Football_Championship" title="Ulster Senior Football Championship">Ulster Senior Football Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Senior_Hurling_Championship" title="Ulster Senior Hurling Championship">Ulster Senior Hurling Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/All-Ireland_Senior_Football_Championship" title="All-Ireland Senior Football Championship">All-Ireland Senior Football Championship</a> and <a href="/wiki/All-Ireland_Senior_Hurling_Championship" title="All-Ireland Senior Hurling Championship">All-Ireland Senior Hurling Championship</a>.\n</p><p>Recent successes for Northern Ireland teams include <a href="/wiki/Armagh_GAA" title="Armagh GAA">Armagh</a>\'s 2002 <a href="/wiki/All-Ireland_Senior_Football_Championship" title="All-Ireland Senior Football Championship">All-Ireland Senior Football Championship</a> win and <a href="/wiki/Tyrone_GAA" title="Tyrone GAA">Tyrone</a>\'s wins in 2003, 2005 and 2008.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golf">Golf</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_(crowd,_landscape_orientation).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_%28crowd%2C_landscape_orientation%29.jpg/220px-Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_%28crowd%2C_landscape_orientation%29.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_%28crowd%2C_landscape_orientation%29.jpg/330px-Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_%28crowd%2C_landscape_orientation%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_%28crowd%2C_landscape_orientation%29.jpg/440px-Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_%28crowd%2C_landscape_orientation%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rory_McIlroy_watches_drive_flight_(crowd,_landscape_orientation).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prominent Northern Irish golfer <a href="/wiki/Rory_McIlroy" title="Rory McIlroy">Rory McIlroy</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Perhaps Northern Ireland\'s most notable successes in professional sport have come in golf. Northern Ireland has contributed more major champions in the modern era than any other European country, with three in the space of just 14 months from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Open_(golf)" title="U.S. Open (golf)">US Open</a> in 2010 to <a href="/wiki/The_Open_Championship" title="The Open Championship">The Open Championship</a> in 2011. Notable golfers include <a href="/wiki/Fred_Daly_(golfer)" title="Fred Daly (golfer)">Fred Daly</a> (winner of The Open in 1947), Ryder Cup players <a href="/wiki/Ronan_Rafferty" title="Ronan Rafferty">Ronan Rafferty</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Feherty" title="David Feherty">David Feherty</a>, leading European Tour professionals <a href="/wiki/David_Jones_(golfer)" title="David Jones (golfer)">David Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hoey_(golfer)" title="Michael Hoey (golfer)">Michael Hoey</a> (a winner on Tour in 2011) and <a href="/wiki/Gareth_Maybin" title="Gareth Maybin">Gareth Maybin</a>, as well as three recent major winners <a href="/wiki/Graeme_McDowell" title="Graeme McDowell">Graeme McDowell</a> (winner of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Open_(golf)" title="U.S. Open (golf)">US Open</a> in 2010, the first European to do so since 1970), <a href="/wiki/Rory_McIlroy" title="Rory McIlroy">Rory McIlroy</a> (winner of four <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_major_golf_championships" title="Men&#39;s major golf championships">majors</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Darren_Clarke" title="Darren Clarke">Darren Clarke</a> (winner of The Open in 2011).<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Northern Ireland has also contributed several players to the Great Britain and Ireland <a href="/wiki/Walker_Cup" title="Walker Cup">Walker Cup</a> team, including <a href="/wiki/Alan_Dunbar" title="Alan Dunbar">Alan Dunbar</a> and Paul Cutler who played on the victorious 2011 team in Scotland.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Golfing_Union_of_Ireland" title="Golfing Union of Ireland">Golfing Union of Ireland</a>, the governing body for men\'s and boy\'s amateur golf throughout Ireland and the oldest golfing union in the world, was founded in Belfast in 1891. Northern Ireland\'s golf courses include the Royal Belfast Golf Club (the earliest, formed in 1881), <a href="/wiki/Royal_Portrush_Golf_Club" title="Royal Portrush Golf Club">Royal Portrush Golf Club</a>, which is the only course outside Great Britain to have hosted <a href="/wiki/The_Open_Championship" title="The Open Championship">The Open Championship</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Royal_County_Down_Golf_Club" title="Royal County Down Golf Club">Royal County Down Golf Club</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Golf_Digest" title="Golf Digest">Golf Digest</a></i> magazine\'s top-rated course outside the United States).<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Snooker">Snooker</span></h3>\n<p>Northern Ireland has produced two world <a href="/wiki/Snooker" title="Snooker">snooker</a> champions; <a href="/wiki/Alex_Higgins" title="Alex Higgins">Alex Higgins</a>, who won the title in 1972 and 1982, and <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Taylor" title="Dennis Taylor">Dennis Taylor</a>, who won in 1985. The highest-ranked Northern Ireland professional on the world circuit presently is <a href="/wiki/Mark_Allen_(snooker_player)" title="Mark Allen (snooker player)">Mark Allen</a> from <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">Antrim</a>. The sport is governed locally by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Billiards_and_Snooker_Association" title="Northern Ireland Billiards and Snooker Association">Northern Ireland Billiards and Snooker Association</a> who run regular ranking tournaments and competitions.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor_sports">Motor sports</span></h3>\n<p>Although Northern Ireland lacks an international automobile racecourse, two Northern Irish drivers have finished inside the top two of <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a>, with <a href="/wiki/John_Watson_(racing_driver)" title="John Watson (racing driver)">John Watson</a> achieving the feat in <a href="/wiki/1982_Formula_One_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1982 Formula One season">1982</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Irvine" title="Eddie Irvine">Eddie Irvine</a> doing the same in <a href="/wiki/1999_Formula_One_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1999 Formula One season">1999</a>. The largest course and the only <a href="/wiki/Motor_Sports_Association" title="Motor Sports Association">MSA</a>-licensed track for UK-wide competition is <a href="/wiki/Kirkistown_Circuit" title="Kirkistown Circuit">Kirkistown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rugby_league">Rugby league</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ireland_national_rugby_league_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland national rugby league team">Ireland national rugby league team</a> has participated in the <a href="/wiki/Rugby_League_Emerging_Nations_Tournament" title="Rugby League Emerging Nations Tournament">Emerging Nations Tournament</a> (1995), the Super League World Nines (1996), the World Cup (2000 and 2008), European Nations Cup (since 2003) and Victory Cup (2004).\n</p><p>The Ireland A rugby league team compete annually in the Amateur Four Nations competition (since 2002) and the St Patrick\'s Day Challenge (since 1995).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_wrestling">Professional wrestling</span></h3>\n<p>In 2007, after the closure of UCW (Ulster Championship Wrestling) which was a wrestling promotion, PWU formed, standing for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pro_Wrestling_Ulster&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pro Wrestling Ulster (page does not exist)">Pro Wrestling Ulster</a>. The wrestling promotion features championships, former <a href="/wiki/List_of_WWE_personnel" title="List of WWE personnel">WWE superstars</a> and local independent wrestlers. Events and IPPV\'s throughout Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Education in Northern Ireland">Education in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belfast_(455),_October_2009.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Belfast_%28455%29%2C_October_2009.JPG/220px-Belfast_%28455%29%2C_October_2009.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Belfast_%28455%29%2C_October_2009.JPG/330px-Belfast_%28455%29%2C_October_2009.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Belfast_%28455%29%2C_October_2009.JPG/440px-Belfast_%28455%29%2C_October_2009.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belfast_(455),_October_2009.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University_Belfast" title="Queen&#39;s University Belfast">Queen\'s University Belfast</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Unlike most areas of the United Kingdom, in the last year of primary school, many children sit entrance examinations for <a href="/wiki/Grammar_schools_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammar schools in the United Kingdom">grammar schools</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Integrated_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Education">Integrated schools</a>, which attempt to ensure a balance in enrolment between pupils of Protestant, Roman Catholic and other faiths (or none), are becoming increasingly popular, although Northern Ireland still has a primarily <i>de facto</i> religiously segregated education system. In the primary school sector, forty schools (8.9% of the total number) are integrated schools and thirty-two (7.2% of the total number) are <a href="/wiki/Gaelscoileanna" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelscoileanna">Irish language-medium schools</a>.\n</p><p>The main universities in Northern Ireland are <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_University_Belfast" title="Queen&#39;s University Belfast">Queen\'s University Belfast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_University" title="Ulster University">Ulster University</a>, and the distance learning <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a> which has a regional office in Belfast.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife">Wildlife</span></h2>\n<p>Algae/seaweed.\n356 species of marine <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> have been recorded in the north-east of Ireland. As Counties Londonderry, Antrim and Down are the only three counties of Northern Ireland with a shoreline this will apply to all Northern Ireland. 77 species are considered rare having been recorded rarely.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_communications">Media and communications</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg/220px-BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg/330px-BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg/440px-BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BBC_Northern_Ireland_Belfast.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Broadcasting_House,_Belfast" title="Broadcasting House, Belfast">Broadcasting House, Belfast</a>, home of <a href="/wiki/BBC_Northern_Ireland" title="BBC Northern Ireland">BBC Northern Ireland</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> has a division called <a href="/wiki/BBC_Northern_Ireland" title="BBC Northern Ireland">BBC Northern Ireland</a> with headquarters in Belfast. As well as broadcasting standard UK-wide programmes, BBC NI produces local content, including a news break-out called <a href="/wiki/BBC_Newsline" title="BBC Newsline">BBC Newsline</a>. The <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">ITV</a> franchise in Northern Ireland is <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Television">Ulster Television</a> (UTV). The state-owned <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> and the privately owned <a href="/wiki/Channel_5_(UK)" title="Channel 5 (UK)">Channel 5</a> also broadcast in Northern Ireland. Access is available to satellite and cable services.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> All Northern Ireland viewers must obtain a UK TV licence to watch live television transmissions.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Raidi%C3%B3_Teilif%C3%ADs_%C3%89ireann" title="Raidi\xc3\xb3 Teilif\xc3\xads \xc3\x89ireann">RT\xc3\x89</a>, the national broadcaster of the Republic of Ireland, is available over the air to most parts of Northern Ireland via reception overspill<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> and via satellite and cable.  Since the digital TV switchover, <a href="/wiki/RT%C3%89_One" title="RT\xc3\x89 One">RT\xc3\x89 One</a>, <a href="/wiki/RT%C3%892" title="RT\xc3\x892">RT\xc3\x892</a> and the Irish-language channel <a href="/wiki/TG4" title="TG4">TG4</a>, are now available over the air on the UK\'s Freeview system from transmitters within Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Although they are transmitted in standard definition, a Freeview HD box or television is required for reception.\n</p><p>As well as the standard UK-wide radio stations from the BBC, Northern Ireland is home to many local radio stations, such as <a href="/wiki/Cool_FM" title="Cool FM">Cool FM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belfast_CityBeat" title="Belfast CityBeat">CityBeat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Q102.9" title="Q102.9">Q102.9</a>. The BBC has two regional radio stations which broadcast in Northern Ireland, <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Ulster" title="BBC Radio Ulster">BBC Radio Ulster</a> and <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Foyle" title="BBC Radio Foyle">BBC Radio Foyle</a>.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Belfast_Telegraph" title="Belfast Telegraph">Belfast Telegraph</a></i> is the leading newspaper, and UK and Irish national newspapers are also available. There is a range of local newspapers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/The_News_Letter" title="The News Letter">News Letter</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Irish_News" title="The Irish News">Irish News</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Northern Ireland uses the same telecommunications and postal services as the rest of the United Kingdom at standard domestic rates and there are no mobile roaming charges between Great Britain and Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> People in Northern Ireland who live close to the border with the Republic of Ireland may inadvertently switch over to the Irish mobile networks, causing international roaming fees to be applied.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Calls from landlines in Northern Ireland to numbers in the Republic of Ireland are charged at the same rate as those to numbers in Great Britain, while landline numbers in Northern Ireland can similarly be called from the Republic of Ireland at domestic rates, using the <a href="/wiki/Telephone_numbers_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland#Calls_to_Northern_Ireland" title="Telephone numbers in the Republic of Ireland">048</a> prefix.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist Dunn; H. Dawson (2000), <i>An Alphabetical Listing of Word, Name and Place in Northern Ireland and the Living Language of Conflict</i>, Lampeter: Edwin Mellen Press, <q>One specific problem \xe2\x80\x93 in both general and particular senses \xe2\x80\x93 is to know what to call Northern Ireland itself: in the general sense, it is not a country, or a province, or a state \xe2\x80\x93 although some refer to it contemptuously as a statelet: the least controversial word appears to be jurisdiction, but this might change.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=An+Alphabetical+Listing+of+Word%2C+Name+and+Place+in+Northern+Ireland+and+the+Living+Language+of+Conflict&amp;rft.place=Lampeter&amp;rft.pub=Edwin+Mellen+Press&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.au=S.+Dunn&amp;rft.au=H.+Dawson&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANorthern+Ireland" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-interpretingNI-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-interpretingNI_12-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFJ._WhyteG._FitzGerald1991" class="citation">J. Whyte; G. FitzGerald (1991), <i>Interpreting Northern Ireland</i>, Oxford: Oxford University Press, <q>One problem must be adverted to in writing about Northern Ireland. This is the question of what name to give to the various geographical entities. These names can be controversial, with the choice often revealing one\'s political preferences. ... some refer to Northern Ireland as a \'province\'. That usage can arouse irritation particularly among nationalists, who claim the title \'province\' should be properly reserved to the four historic provinces of Ireland-Ulster, Leinster, Munster, and Connacht. If I want to a label to apply to Northern Ireland I shall call it a \'region\'. Unionists should find that title as acceptable as \'province\': Northern Ireland appears as a region in the regional statistics of the United Kingdom published by the British government.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Interpreting+Northern+Ireland&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.au=J.+Whyte&amp;rft.au=G.+FitzGerald&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANorthern+Ireland" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-placeApart-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-placeApart_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-placeApart_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-placeApart_13-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-placeApart_13-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-placeApart_13-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-placeApart_13-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFD._Murphy1979" class="citation">D. Murphy (1979), <i>A Place Apart</i>, London: Penguin Books, <q>Next \xe2\x80\x93 what noun is appropriate to Northern Ireland? \'Province\' won\'t do since one-third of the province is on the wrong side of the border. \'State\' implies more self-determination than Northern Ireland has ever had and \'country\' or \'nation\' are blatantly absurd. \'Colony\' has overtones that would be resented by both communities and \'statelet\' sounds too patronizing, though outsiders might consider it more precise than anything else; so one is left with the unsatisfactory word \'region\'.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Place+Apart&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pub=Penguin+Books&amp;rft.date=1979&amp;rft.au=D.+Murphy&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANorthern+Ireland" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-gfa-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-gfa_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFGovernment_of_the_United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Northern_IrelandGovernment_of_Ireland1998" class="citation">Government of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland; Variable:BRITISH.</a> Summary: 78% of Protestants replied "Strongly British."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ark.ac.uk/nilt/1999/Community_Relations/IRISH.html">Northern Ireland Life and Times Survey, 1999; Module:Community Relations, Variable:IRISH</a> Summary: 77% of Catholics replied "Strongly Irish."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Institute of Governance, 2006 <i>"National identities in the UK: do they matter?"</i>\nBriefing No. 16, January 2006; Retrieved from <cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060822152404/http://www.institute-of-governance.org/forum/Leverhulme/briefing_pdfs/IoG_Briefing_16.pdf">"IoG_Briefing"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Conversely, a majority of Catholics (65%) regard themselves as Irish, whilst very few Protestants (5%) do likewise. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 November</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Northern+Ireland+Assembly+logo&amp;rft.pub=Niassembly.gov.uk&amp;rft.au=Northern+Ireland+Assembly+Information+Office&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.niassembly.gov.uk%2Fio%2Flogo.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANorthern+Ireland" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFAlan_O&#39;Day1987" class="citation">Alan O\'Day, ed. (1987), <i>Reactions To Irish Nationalism, 1865\xe2\x80\x931914</i>, London: Hambledon Press, <q>With the Rising of 1916 a break took place in national symbolism which was most visibly manifested in the national flag and the anthem which the young Irish nation accepted. The demise of the Parliamentary Party stands in direct parallel to the just as rapidly diminishing power of its symbols. The green flag and \'God save Ireland\' began to be discredited as symbols of constitutional nationalism and, instead, the symbols of revolutionary nationalism gained popularity as the majority of the Irish people identified itself with the political aims of the Easter revolutionaries. The use of symbols made apparent that the occurrences of 1916 initiated a new epoch in Irish history much in the same as the Union of 1801 and the Famine of 1845\xe2\x80\x938 did.<p>Both the national flag and the national anthem of present-day Ireland drive origins directly from the Rising. At first it still appeared as if the revolutionaries would take over the old symbols because on the roof of their headquarters, the Dublin General Post Office, a green flag with the harp was hoisted next to the republican tricolour although with the inscription \'Irish Republic\'. Even \'Got save Ireland\' was sung by the revolutionaries during Easter week. But after the failure of the Rising and the subsequent executions of the leading revolutionaries the tricolour and \'The Soldier\'s Song\' became more and more popular as symbols of the rebellion.</p></q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Reactions+To+Irish+Nationalism%2C+1865%E2%80%931914&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pub=Hambledon+Press&amp;rft.date=1987&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANorthern+Ireland" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-137"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-137">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/uk_news/northern_ireland/7748005.stm">Vandals curbed by plastic edging</a> BBC News, 25 November 2008.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-138"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-138">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.opsi.gov.uk/sr/sr2000/20000347.htm">"Statutory Rule 2000 No. 347"</a>.
